B0034YXY5C|3.0|My first thought when setting out to write this review was, 'Hmmm, I wonder how many irrelevant and obscure titles I can work into the review, that may or may not shed some light on the subject matter at hand?'<br /><br />I am a fairly well-read person (I am told I am too modest, and that I qualify as a very well read person, but I don't want to claim too much, lest some diabolic force leap forward to claim me back for my sin of pride, a particularly bad thing for those of holy orders, as every guide and advice to the priestly states since the days of Irenaeus). But I digress.<br /><br />But this is in keeping with the flavour of the novel. Unless you like digression, stay far away (run, don't walk!) from this novel. But, if you go in for the seemingly endless, barely understandable and eventually irrelevant, then by all means, this is the book for you.<br /><br />I would not have read this book but for the urgings of friends who were similarly reading this, and wanted to compare notes. Thanks, guys!<br /><br />Now, I must hasten to add at this point, I did find myself enjoying the book several times. I don't mind the digressions, but I found the attempts (subtle but there) to equate Corso with a Holmesian character unconvincing (and the attempts were subtle enough that Perez-Reverte could claim they aren't actually there). Apart from Holmes, he ignores most of English literature--when he talks of those who wrote in serial form, i.e, Stendahl et alia, he neglects those such as Dickens.<br /><br />I would love to have the card catalogue Perez-Reverte was sitting next to as he wrote this; perhaps he used Amazon.com and other internet searching to add that final irrelevant entry in each of the listings of books.<br /><br />I am a bibliophile. I sit in a room, such as Perez-Reverte describes in several places, where shelves sag on walls groaning from the strain of heavy books, heavy in content as well as mass weight. I have several such rooms in my home. But I couldn't quite find the same love of books in this book, where sometimes bibliophilia was a fetish, and other times a useless detail.<br /><br />Basic plot--is it a murder or is it a suicide? What does the manuscript have to do with it? Dumas (who wrote, among other things, the stories involving the Musketeers) is seemingly at the centre of things, along with a possibly-phoney book of occult forbidden knowledge, for which men will die (obviously) or kill. There is diabolic force at work, maybe. Corso is the rare-books expert called in to investigate the case, who doesn't always like the role of policeman.<br /><br />Perez-Reverte seems intent on impressing us with his erudition in the field of literature and continental travel. It would be impressive if it were consistent and organised along better lines, and woven into a story where such details made a contribution. This book, as a mystery, fails to deliver because it both gives more information than is necessary (which can be an effective device, forcing the reader to separate 'the wheat from the chaff', or figure out which strands to follow; sadly, that is not the case here) and insufficient information in the strands which count to allow the reader to 'figure it out' (another sin, often difficult to avoid when one is trying for a 'complex' novel; even Conan Doyle frequently failed on this point).<br /><br />In all, this is an inventive idea, with interesting character formation; for some reason, neither the story nor the characters get developed sufficiently (either in size or direction) to make this a first-class novel.<br /><br />The New York Daily News described this book as 'A cross between Umberto Eco and Anne Rice' (Eco is very prominent here, as Perez-Reverte is obviously trying to emulate and cash in on Eco's prominence in the richer-and-book-purchasing English-speaking world). The New Yorker gets even more, well, New-Yorker-ish: 'A noir metafiction [please someone, tell me just what that means, in English??] Even a reader armed with a Latin dictionary and a copy of The Three Musketeers cannot anticipate the thrilling twists of this stylish, Escher-like mystery.'<br /><br />Perhaps one of the reasons one cannot anticipate 'the thrilling twists' is the inconsistency in the novel? Just a thought.<br /><br />Escher-like...well, yes, you read one thing and it turns out to be another (for instance, I thought I was reading a great novel, and it turned out to be...)<br /><br />No, that is unfair. It is worth reading, but not devoting much time toward. Use as a diversion, not a devotional. I won't spoil the ending for you. I don't like it when others do that for me. You'll have to suffer as I and my fellow readers did to find out. You may love it! You may despise it! Yes, it is one of those books.teers cannot anticipate the thrilling twists of this stylish, Escher-like mystery.' I realize that creating a rich fantasy fiction world takes a lot of effort, but there are far too many \\"epics\\" in fantasy fiction today.    I hope that Rothfuss will have the discipline to finish the story in book 3 rather than setting up a second trilogy or series, which no doubt his publisher strongly desires. I stand ready to be awed.

Suzanne Collins knows that some kids have \\"weak stomachs\\" and makes sure to balance out the action and gore in Catching Fire. For example, whenever Maysilee is attacked by vicious birds, Collins simply says the birds pierced her neck then goes on to talk about something less gory, but just as interesting.  Also, Collins does a great job expressing Katniss's personality and emotions throughout the book. Whenever Katniss is talking about Finnick Odair, a District 4 victor, she not only describes him, but gives her own opinion of him. This makes the book fun to read, because it's not just boring descriptions and an occasional opinion, you get to read the whole thing through Katniss's eyes. Whenever Katniss finds out she will be in the Games again, the emotions are much stronger and real than when the author just says, \\"Katniss then found out she was going to be in the Hunger Games again.\\" It makes you feel like you are right there next to Katniss. Overall, anybody who picks up this book will not want to put it down.

Some artists go off and do \\"theme\\" albums in their career, be it a dance or covers album or in the 70's for instance everyone did a disco album at some point. And that's what this is for me, a total change of pace. It can't be compared to her previous albums because it's nothing like those albums. \\"In Your Dreams\\" is a breath of brand new air from Stevie. I've always felt that \\"Bella Donna\\" was Stevie's masterpiece, and it remains so, but \\"In Your Dreams\\" is also a masterpiece but painted in a very different style with very different colors. The stand out tracks are:  \\"In Your Dreams\\" ~ I have a feeling Stevie really likes this song and I bet this ends up in the set this summer. It's just one of  those fast up tempo songs that she likes doing on stage.     \\"New Orleans\\" ~ Starts off slow, but then the chorus kicks in and it blooms.     \\"Moonlight (A Vampire's Dream)\\" ~ This is where the album kicks in for me. The beginning is taken from an old demo fans have had for years called \\"Lady From The Mountain\\" and she sings it almost exactly like the demo in the whisper like voice. She's turned a lot of older beloved demos into full-fledged songs lately and some of them didn't work, \\"Smile At You\\", \\"Goodbye Baby\\", but this one does. The new lyrics added to the original track  really complete and fit the song.     \\"Annabel Lee\\" ~ This was demo'd for 2001's \\"Trouble In Shangri-La\\" but didn't make the cut.  Music by Stevie, lyrics based on the poem By Edgar Allan Poe.  Stevie and Poe seem to be a match made in heaven.     \\"Soldier's Angel\\" ~ First thought was this was another \\"Desert Angel\\" (a track from her 1991 best of album, \\"TimeSpace\\") but this is much better. Lindsey Buckingham adds a sparse, cold guitar and vocals that just nail this song. Thisisn't supposed to be a happy song, it's dark, depressing, cold, and empty. It's a perfect example of music and subject matter being perfectly matched. Not a song that everyone will like, but the song is almost like a slap in the face. Stevie makes her point beautifully.     \\"Ghosts Are Gone\\" ~ This would be a perfect song to open her show with. This is one of those songs that you cannot sit still and just listen to.     \\"\\"Italian Summer\\" ~ The big lush strings at the start are a switch for Stevie. Another nice touch are the Beach Boy-esque background \\"aahh ahhh's\\" Again the music fits the lyric and you feel like you're out there with her looking out over the ocean at the islands. And that final note? Who knew Stevie had that in her!!??    \\"Cheaper Than Free\\" ~ A simple country song with simple yet thought provoking lyrics.     Stevie is back and better than ever on \\"In Your Dreams\\". Longtime fans will love this album, casual fans may need some time to digest it. It's a different sound for Stevie, but still unmistakably Stevie.

Here's the problem: Neo-folk, as best I understand it, is perhaps most significant for being a genre of music that has never, nor will ever, attempt to exist. When Fleet Foxes or The Decemberists put down \\"The Unbearable Lightness of Being\\" just long enough to pick up a banjo and a jug and sing a song about their heartfelt Christian beliefs or how hard it is to chop down a tree, we are to immediately assume they are speaking metaphorically. It is his methodological reasoning and perception in staying ahead of various enemies that stands out. For a while there, it seemed like peace and co-operation between nations was about to break out.  In place of not having any credible choices of an enemy to externalize, the US Government turned to demonization of internal enemies, such as patriot and paramilitary groups. Usually shown as a bunch of bearded crazies ranting about the constitution and wanting to secede from the Union. So author Lee Child attempts to cash in here on this mid 90's trend.    But the problem is the issues Lee Child tries to ridicule, such as the federal reserve, constitutional violations, ever omnipotent Government....these are real and serious issues, and books like 'Die Trying' seek to tar those who are concerned with such matters, by association with armed crazies in fatigues. In fact, the people really concerned about such matters are mostly non-violent types respectful of other folk's liberty and property. Many such people are naturally and unknowingly following the 'non-aggression principle' (google the term)    So.....     The way Lee Child and the real US administration of the time hyped this Patriot threat was to be the biggest joke of all; as if a bunch of fat guys with guns under their beds are going to form some sort of Redneck A-team and overthrow the Government. It was a pure fantasy threat to the power of Government, just a convenient excuse to expand the reach of the State and violate ever more of our freedoms and liberties.    You may see this review as going off on a tangent, but do not get me wrong.......even if I had no problem with the politics, this book is still bad (1 star) because of the first mentioned reasons; no investigative work, running through the jungle like Rambo, and comical villains (comical even by the standards of Jack Reacher novels).

0756404738|2.0|The Wise Man's Fears suffers from a problem that has been getting out of control in the publishing business: lack of editing.  It seems that the effects of the huge layoffs over the last few years are becoming more and more obvious.  The average number of pages in books, especially in the science fiction/fantasy genre, has grown enormously without an equal increase in quality.  This book could have been as great as The Name of the Wind if someone had spent time cutting out about a third of it and making sure that the story was actually compelling. I too would find it difficult to throw away pages that I had spent hundreds of hours composing. That's what editors are for.  I don't expect every editor to be Maxwell Perkins, but some sort of discipline has to be imposed. B004L3AQFG|5.0|It's a question that the Saw film franchise has been asking for seven films: how far would you go to survive? Would you harm a stranger, or someone you loved? Would you do the nigh unthinkable and cut off a limb? 127 Hours asks this as well, but with two big exceptions: The first is that it's based on a true story, and second, it's actually good.    Directed by Danny Boyle (28 Days Later, Trainspotting, Slumdog Millionaire), 127 Hours tells the true story of Aron Ralston, a hiker and canyoneer who, in 2003, was hiking in Utah's Blue John canyon when his arm became trapped under a heavy boulder. After being trapped for five days, Ralston amputated his right arm using a dull pocket knife and walked out of the canyon.    Based on Ralston's book, Between a Rock and a Hard Place, the film faced an extremely difficult challenge: how does one make a feature length film where the story boils down to \\"guy trapped in a canyon for a long time\\"? It's a challenge that Danny Boyle, and more impressively, star James Franco (Milk, Spider-Man) meet head on. In front of the camera, Franco hits the mark almost like Robin Hood: from goofy adventurer to seriously considering the very immediate issue of his mortality, his performance never once descends into the realm of camp.    What really helps to pull the film together is the editing, balanced between beautiful wide shots of Utah's landscapes, up close and personal shots of James Franco, and music video-like hallucinations, with many shots cut together in quick succession to not only condense what is going on, but to ramp up the intensity of the moment as well. The film's editors are very skilled, piecing together the various elements without ever once giving it the feeling that there is a case of Dissociative Personality Disorder going on, nor slowing down the film's break-neck pace.     Where the film could have gone horrendously wrong was the amputation scene. The scene is cut together very quickly, and never lingers on any graphic image for too long. The soundtrack really amps up the intensity, and when it was all over, I found myself holding my breath for the duration. The whole scene was done in one take with multiple cameras and took about twenty minutes to shoot, and is condensed to a brief two to three minute montage. Just be careful if you decide to take hold of someone's hand during this part - you may very well end up breaking it.     There is a slight problem with character development, or lack thereof. During Ralston's many hallucinations, he is taunted by images of his ex-girlfriend (Clmence Posy). It's apparent that their relationship ended badly, and we are shown the how, but never the why. Several times we also \\"see\\" a number of people sitting on a couch observing Ralston, but only a handful of them are ever identified: namely his parents, sister, and co-worker. Who are the other ten or so people, and why are they so important that we see them?     127 Hours was given a wide release just in time for the awards season, and received six Academy Award nominations, including Best Picture, Best Actor, and Best Adapted Screenplay. Regardless of how many golden statues those involved take home on February 27, 127 Hours is a very strong film: part documentary, part drama, part thriller, all held together with an excellent performance by James Franco and another great piece of work by director Danny Boyle. Running a scant 90 minutes, it never outstays its welcome, and the frequent trips into Ralston's mind held break up the monotony of being stuck in a canyon. It certainly won't appeal to everyone, but then again, what does?

0307272702|3.0|Taubes asks good questions, but is intellectually dishonest in some answers.    Mr Taubes certainly asks the right questions. Why are we getting fat, even in populations that seemingly don't have enough food? Why are diets hard to stick to? Why is exercise not automatically going to assist in weight loss? These, and many other questions, are very pertinent. Taubes does a decent job of reviewing \\"some\\" of the research literature to bring the need to show these questions.    But his answers are weak. There are many that I could see, and I'm sure others that I'm not knowledgeable enough address / correct that others can.    1) For instance, Taubes tries to discredit the importance the Energy Balance equation in weight loss. He says that it's not calories in and out that matter. He then goes on to discuss how fat is gained first, which then forces the body to demand more intake of food. Or decreased in activity. And he uses this as proof that calories don't matter. Actually, it further proofs that they do!    First, you CANNOT have a change in MASS of your body without an increase in MASS consumed vs MASS burned (oxidized). This is really, really, fundamental. You can't create mass from nothing. Taubes is correct that you can first convert some mass to fat, then as for more mass to be taken in, so that the cause / effect are reversed, but even still, mass balance is still a law.    Energy balance (or thermodynamics if you want) law basically says Energy In (food) = Metabolic rate + Food Breakdown Energy + Subconscious Energy (aka NEAT, nervous energy) + Exercise + Change in Weight    **ATTENTION** This is a CONSTRAINT. It is not the ONLY constraint. Taubes illustrates that there are OTHER constraints too (such as, if you exercise more, you are going to tend to eat more as well). But that does not discredit the energy balance constraint. And all the controlled research verifies this.     So Taubes says, 'energy balance doesn't matter', 'carbs matter'. FALSE. Energy balance does matter, and yes maybe carbs matter too. Again, multiple constraints.    This fundamental communication really matters, because people tend to look at things as black/white, and if they read \\"calories don't matter\\", well they propagate that and interpret as \\"I can eat as much as I want\\", which is false.     2) Taubes attempts to simply the fat storage / release problem to simply insulin (mentioning only briefly some other hormones that play small roles). But wait, he doesn't mention LEPTIN???? Google \\"Leptin\\". How can you have a book on weight regulation without mentioning leptin?

B004HO6I4M|4.0|The best single word I can use to describe the film Rio is 'vibrant' as it's the colors that really hit you.  One can see why, when you're doing a story about birds, setting it in Brazil with its dazzling variety of brilliantly multicolored avian species is a truly inspired choice.<br /><br />The story begins when a baby blue macaw in the Brazilian rain forest falls (literally) into the hands of exotic animal poachers and ends up being shipped to the US where, by a fortunate accident, he ends up being adopted by a young girl named Linda in a small town in, of all places, wintry Minnesota.  A quick flash forward shows girl and bird, which she names Blu, growing up together, to fifteen years later where Linda is now an adult who owns her own bookstore. The thing is though, Blu, raised as a house-bird, never learned how to fly.<br /><br />The plot really begins when a Brazilian ornithologist named Tulio comes to Linda's store, having learned from her web-site that she's the owner of Blu, who apparently is one of the last male blue macaws in the world. Despite getting off on the wrong foot with Linda, he eventually persuades her to come with him to Brazil so that they can mate Blu with a female blue macaw named Jewel that they've managed to capture. Once there, however, the mating plan hits a snag as Jewel wants only to escape back to her jungle and has nothing but disdain for Blu, who only wants to get back with his human.  Further complications set in when both birds are stolen by exotic animal black-marketeers who intend to sell them to the highest bidder. The two birds, though chained together, manage to escape, but it's rough going, particularly when Jewel discovers that Blu can't fly and she can't fly away while she's chained to him, and the bird thieves are hot on their trail, right in the middle of Brazil's biggest and most flamboyant of all festivals, Carnival.<br /><br />Director/writer Carlos Saldanha goes the distance in bringing some authentic Brazilian touches to the film. I particularly liked the way in which he brought out how everyone in Brazil is soccer-crazy, with good guys and bad constantly being distracted whenever a game is on.<br /><br />The themes are familiar ones - characters are thrust out of their familiar environments into ones they find strange and challenging, characters who don't get along but are stuck together learn to appreciate each other, danger threatens, help comes from unexpected quarters, fears are overcome, and so on - but the characters in Rio are engaging and the story-telling is well done so you just lean back and enjoy the show. And Rio is a true visual pleasure to watch just for the sheer vibrancy of its colors and for the exotic setting of Rio de Janeiro during Carnival.<br /><br />The voice actors for the most part do a creditable job bringing their characters to life. The stand-outs are clearly Jesse Eisenberg (The Social Network) as Blu and Anne Hathaway (The Devil Wears Prada) as Jewel, who succeed in not only bringing out the individual personalities of the two macaws but also make you believe in the way the two birds gradually begin to accept and relate to each other. But Leslie Mann and Rodrigo Santoro are also exceptional as the humans Linda and Tulio, Mann for bringing out Linda's mix of independence and introvert and Santoro for Tulio's engagingly manic if clumsy extrovert.  Comedic actor Tracy Morgan has fun voicing Luiz, a Carnival-loving bulldog whose bark may be worse than his bite but whose drool trumps both. And Jermaine Clement (Flight of the Conchords) adds a deliciously nasty touch as the villainous cockatoo, Nigel.<br /><br />(Note: I was wondering why a Brazilian cockatoo was talking with what sounded like an Australian accent, but I later found out that cockatoos are in fact of Australasian origin. So chalk up another point for Rio for being educational as well as entertaining.)<br /><br />The songs are enjoyable if largely forgettable but nicely add to the Brazilian atmosphere. The stand-out exception is the song Nigel sings (as near as I can tell, it's simply called \\"Nigel's Song\\") where he explains why he is \\"not a pretty bird\\".  There seems to be some kind of rule, in recent years anyway, that in any animated musical film, the bad guys always get the best songs.<br /><br />Highly recommended for anyone who enjoys beautifully colored  animation, engaging characters and good basic storytelling.zilian atmosphere. 0385341008|4.0|I had heard people talking about this book ever since it was published--countless people I know had read it and loved it--so I bought it; but then I found myself passing it by on my TBR (to be read) shelf time and time again because I wasn't sure it was my cup of tea (as it turns out, I misunderstood what the book was about from the descriptions I was given). Then I met a new friend and neighbor and she kept at me about it, saying I must read it. FINALLY, after close to two years gathering dust on my shelf, I decided to read it. I am SO glad I did!<br /><br />This is the story of a closeknit community of homey folks who live on Guernsey, one of the Channel Islands between England and France, and Juliet, an author from London, who becomes interested in the story of Guernsey's 5-year occupation by the Germans during World War II. The entire story is told through letters, notes and telegrams--from the Guernsey folks to Juliet and back and from Juliet to her friends and publisher and back.<br /><br />Some of the Guernsey folks are eccentric, but they are all (save one) good-hearted, warm and welcoming people. They may not be sophisticated, but they are incredibly charming and genuine. The Society from the book's title is a book club they started during the war to keep them entertained, since they were denied any news from the mainland during the 5 years the Germans occupied their beloved island. (The Potato Peel Pie part of the title is a dish invented by one of the Society's members during the war, when food was scarce.)<br /><br />The authors do a marvelous job of blending deeply tragic events that took place during the war (both on Guernsey and in concentration camps elsewhere) with the charm and humor of the Guernsey people. It is not a depressing war story, though it could have been in another author's hands. It is a story filled with folksy humor, tall tales and a community of loving people who are always there for one another, even in the hardest of times.<br /><br />You will fall in love with these characters and this enchanted place they call home. Even the story of how the book was finished was so in keeping with spirit of the story itself. The original author, Mary Ann Shaffer, took ill and asked her niece, children's book author Annie Barrows, to finish to story. Ms. Shaffer died in 2008, the year the book was published in hardback. What a wonderful legacy Ms. Shaffer has left for her family...and for all of us.br />You will fall in love with these characters and this enchanted place they call home. Even the story of how the book was finished was so in keeping with spirit of the story itself. The original author, Mary Ann Shaffer, took ill and asked her niece, children's book author Annie Barrows, to finish to story. Ms. Shaffer died in 2008, the year the book was published in hardback. B0030ZOYHC|5.0|Many fans of the original series might feel conflicted about what to think of an Evangelion reboot. I had my own trepidations, too, since I loved the Neon Genesis series so much for what it was: a cerebral twist of the mech genre. Rest assured, the reboot delivers a deliciously visual new breath of life into an old story. Fans and new viewers, alike, will enjoy 1.11 and the volumes to follow. The characters, beloved and hated, appear with more personality and understanding.  The reboot is much more direct in that aspect; in the original, it was hard to sympathize with some of the characters because their personalities (or lack thereof) develop over the length of the series, and some of them never have a chance to redeem themselves or resolve their problems. A little of that issue was taken care of in the final movie, which was good, but felt like a footnote on the end of a 26 episode series--a series which sorely needed a resolution to wrap up the story and give the viewer a feeling of closure.     The new Evangelion is going to be much more straightforward. It explains things as they're happening rather than recounting the whats and whys in later episodes, while still leaving some mystery as to the purpose of some characters and their motives. It's definitely a series to buy because it's worth owning and re-watching. It is not a re-hash of the old series; it is Evangelion re-imagined--Eva in another dimension. Not to mention, the animation is fantastic.

0439023521|4.0|Over a century from now, North America is a land ravaged by floods and war. Out of the ashes a new nation, Panem, has emerged, consisting of the glorious city known as the Capitol and twelve outlying districts which only exist to provide the Capitol with resources (a thirteenth district was destroyed in a rebellion three-quarters of a century ago). To keep the people in line, the government enforces the Hunger Games, a reality TV show where twenty-four teenage boys and girls must fight one another for survival in a game of wits and strength.<br /><br />Sixteen-year-old Katniss Everdeen volunteers to take the place of her sister when she is selected for the Games. Transported to a hostile wilderness, Katniss must use every bit of her cunning and her training as a hunter to prevail.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is the first book in the trilogy of the same name. First published in 2008, it has become a hugely successful novel, spawning two sequels and a forthcoming movie adaptation, and has won plaudits from both younger readers and adults alike (Stephen King is a noted fan).<br /><br />The premise is unoriginal but Collins nevertheless executes it reasonably well. This is a brisk read where Collins develops the plot and delivers action beats with skill, but not at the expense of characterisation (though, with only two characters of note to develop, this isn't as challenging as it could be). Katniss is an intriguing but more interesting is the way that Collins establishes the motivation and character of Peeta, Katniss's sometimes-ally in the games, since we only see him through Katniss's eyes. Characters outside these two are less well-developed, however.<br /><br />Collins also holds back on exploring the full savagery of the games, perhaps understandably given the target audience. Still, there is the feeling that we more hear about how horrible the games are rather than seeing them in full flow (one tense moment involving mutated dogs aside). In addition, we know very little about the other contestants. A couple get some nice moments in the sun so we feel bad when they die, but generally the focus of the game itself is the mental battle of wills and PR that Katniss and Peeta are playing with the people running the game. This is surprising and considerably more difficult than just showing the contestants offing one another, since this struggle can only by necessity be depicted through one side, since we only have Katniss's POV, so we, like her, can only guess what the people in the Capitol are up to. To Collins' credit she pulls it off, and works in a couple of interesting themes about reality TV, bloodsports, PR and marketing into the bargain.<br /><br />The book does have a potential problem in that it does come off very much like a Battle Royale-lite. Whilst that's not a problem if you've never read or seen Battle Royale, if you have then the weaknesses of The Hunger Games become slightly more apparent. Most notably, whilst Collins' Games are cruel, they don't match the shocking harshness that Royale achieves by simply having all the contestants be in the same school year, meaning they've known one another for years before having to kill one another. In that sense, Koushan Takami scores higher with some of the things he wants to say about youth and teenager-hood being a Darwinian struggle for survival. At the same time, the two works, whilst stemming from the same basic idea, are aimed in rather different directions and Takami benefits from a much greater word-count and a more adult audience to work with, so comparisons between the two are fair only up to a point.<br /><br />Moving on from that, The Hunger Games (****) is a fast-paced, enjoyable read with some interesting (if hardly revelatory) things to say about celebrity and PR, not to mention a counter-intuitive approach to the inevitable romance story, but suffers a little from the well-mined premise and patchy characterisation. The novel is available now in the UK and USA.

0007268149|5.0|Please note that all reviews prior to February 2010 concern the first edition. However, much of what has been said for that first edition holds for the new one as well. And if you live in North America, it makes more sense (economically), to get the Princeton version: Birds of Europe: (Second Edition) (Princeton Field Guides). That edition is not only cheaper, it also has a more suitable name. Its contents is exactly the same, despite the different name.    At any rate, the long awaited second edition of this excellent field guide is finally here. Improving on the world's best bird field guide is almost certainly a task that will disappoint a few. However, the authors and illustrators are to be commended for their achievement. Despite an addition of almost fifty pages, compared to the first edition, this is still a very field-worthy book. The text, range maps, and many illustrations have been throughly revised and updated. Particularly noteworthy are the many new owl illustrations, and the page of \\"Atlantic\\" pigeons. Many \\"new\\" species have been added due to splits from previous subspecies. Thus, many island races from the Canary Islands are now full species. Some taxonomic groups got a particular revision, such as the wheatears and the \\"Herring Gull complex\\". The treatment of the latter has been expanded from less than a double page to three full spreads. With Caspian Gull being fully illustrated in various plumages, for example. The taxonomic sequence got some changes in the first parts of the book, with geese and ducks now at the front. The same confusing fashion as in field guides of North America and many other areas of the world. There is actually no need for a field guide to follow the latest taxonomic insights. Rather, there needs to be a sequence that allows for quickly finding a group of birds. And that would be best served if the basic sequence were kept constant.     Unfortunately, non-native species got a rather worse coverage than in the first edition.Some were relegated to the back of the book such as the locally well established Wood Duck and Mandarin Duck. And even for the European native Ruddy Shelduck, the range presumably due to human releases is not shown on the map. Personally, I consider such omissions a lack of recognizing reality. The same problems, unfortunately, are found in such leading works as the Handbook of the Birds of the World, and likewise for the mammal equivalent.     A welcome change in the range maps is the use of more detailed regional maps for very localised species. Despite the time span of about ten years since the first edition, and the repeated postponement in the publication of this edition for about two years, it seems that there must have been a certain rush at the end. Thus, there are relatively many typos or other minor oversights. No big thing, but somewhat of a bother nevertheless. Hopefully, a large part will be corrected in subsequent printings.     Despite the various points I have criticized here, the book fully merits its five stars. It is just SO good! However, if you already own the first edition, and you use it mainly for local birding, you might want to just keep that edition.

Sure there are the usual fantasy settings and tropes, we like to think everything has to be original but the truth of the matter all new works are influenced by previous works, it's how you take that influence and mold into your own that makes what you have truly unique.    Patrick has taken a fallen world and shelled it out, than through the story fills it in with believeable characters, settings and methods of magic. 0743270754|4.0|This was the first major piece I read about Lincoln, and I'm very glad I picked it up.  Apart from being a fantastic historical account, it was also one of the books President Obama read in preparation for his presidency.  After reading this book, it's very apparent Obama took many cues from Lincoln - even some of his rhetoric (\\"better angels\\").  In that sense, this book offers the reader the opportunity to both learn some fascinating history, and understand our own times better.    I found myself frustrated at several points in the latter half of the book.  There are many instances in which Dr. Goodwin provides a narrative of events as filler.  Such filler may be important in setting context, but often times, it bears no relation to the central tenet of the book, which is Lincoln's high political acumen.  After getting through some big chunks of this book, I'd still be asking myself \\"Okay, how does this relate to Lincoln's thinking?  How does it illustrate his political genius?\\"    Of course, a lot of the time I may have simply been frustrated with Lincoln.  His true political genius - as becomes very apparent in this read - was his ability to remove himself from petty squabbles and maintain a cool head.  This often involved biting his tongue and refraining from reprimanding subordinates and colleagues who were outwardly belligerent.  More often than not, Lincoln's rivals defeated themselves, and it was his wise inaction that prevailed.  I often found myself thinking back to Dale Carnegie's referring to Lincoln in the first chapter of \\"How to Win Friends & Influence People\\", in which he posits that Lincoln was successful because he never reprimanded or belittled those around him, even when he was surely in the right.  \\"Team of Rivals\\" is an extended study of that assertion.

Her voice, her songwriting are all in top form. But we also need to give kudos to Dave Stewart (of The Eurythmics) for his production, and for rekindling Stevie's passion, which is evident throughout this album.   It will probably take me a while to absorb this album as a whole, it's still a collection of songs to me, each individual, with it's own merits, and I am having trouble seeing the Big Picture. That will come with time, I am confident.   The album opens with Secret Love, a song written in 1976. I think it's a good opener, classic Nicks, but nothing special. It eases the listener in, assuring you that, whatever comes next, this is still Stevie Nicks.   For What It's Worth is a worthy successor to 'Landslide' in it's simplicity, honesty, and emotion. Anthony Lower and I have already decided that this is our song.   The title track, is fast and fun. It's also so hopeful. This is a Summer Song, one you listen to while driving around town, with the top down and the speakers blasting.   Now we get funky!! Wide Sargasso Sea is intense, free form, and musically, very different than any other Nicks track. I can't say it's my favorite (to early to tell), but I listen to it the most.  How can you describe New Orleans without using the word 'haunting?' Full of ghosts, vampires, and Anne Rice. Emotionally, it tugs at my heart in a way I cannot describe. Even though it was written for the residents of New Orleans after Katrina hit, it also reminds me of someone very close to me. We bonded over Anne Rice and Julian Mayfair (remember?)  Speaking of vampires, Moonlight (A Vampire's Dream) is a reworking of an old demo (Lady From The Mountain) with a new chorus and a new verse thrown in. Stevie has mentioned that she revisited the song after watching Twilight: New Moon. I forgive her for that, because it's a really good song.   Annabelle Lee is a poem by Edgar Allan Poe. Stevie set it to music years ago. The demo has been floating around for a while, and I've been really hoping she would record it. Much to my delight, the finished product is lovely. Ok, the album might be a whole picture for me yet, but I do love the sequencing of those last 3 songs, they flow together very well.  In 1991, Stevie wrote a song called 'Desert Angel,' dedicated to the troops serving in the first Gulf War. She got a lot of flack from fans, because of the hokey lyrics. I tended to like it, but I live in the desert. I think the idea she had in writing that song was never fully realized, until she recorded Soldier's Angel for this album. Simply done, it's just Stevie, accompanied by Lindsey Buckingham on guitar and vocals (maybe some drums thrown in). Heartbreaking tribute to the men and women and what they have seen and experienced, though Stevie's eyes.   Everybody Loves You the requisite Stevie Nicks song about how hard it is to be a Rock Star, but so well done, it makes you ignore her other forays into this territory.  Ghosts are Gone is Angry Stevie. It's a hard rocker, with a great beat. I found myself playing air tambourine!!  If there is a weak link in this album, You May Be The One is it. It's not necessarily a bad song, I think it just doesn't match up to the eloquence of the rest of the songs.   I had a conversation last night with someone about the stages of love, and the last two tracks really hit on some of the things we discussed. Italian Summer is full and rich and so full of romance. You can almost smell the roses and feel the spray of the Mediterranean on your face as you listen to it. It's Big, It's Spectacular, It's So Soulful. It's all about Romance...  Cheaper Than Free is that other kind of love song. It's a great duet about the simplicity of real love. No strings, no roses, nothing but the expressions of how good it is to have someone you love in your life. It's a perfect closer to the album.      What's brighter than a smile? You child.

It's just not strong enough to fuel the fear in the government that the novel implies.  4) Katniss' tolerance of her fashion entourage from the Capital is utterly baffling. All that time they were plucking and tweezing and primping her, she could have been telling them about the real living conditions she and her district endure back home. Yet she was surprisingly subdued for a girl known for speaking her mind.    This book seriously tested my ability to suspend disbelief.    Having said that, there was one thing that was refreshing: A young female protagonist who is good in a fight. We need more of these. Unfortunately, the world she inhabited was so unbelievable in my mind that I'm afraid she got lost in the soup sandwich mess that is Panem.

B0041KKYBA|2.0|This documentary was a wasted opportunity.  It portends that a massive crime was perpetrated on the country in the crisis, but it fails to name the crime other than the tangential use of prostitutes and drugs by some bankers.  Not to minimize the crime of prostitution, but, that whole bit was so completely asinine, it shows the lack of understanding that the film makers had of the issues at stake in the crisis.  The fact is that no laws appear to have been broken in arriving at the crisis.  The banks generally acted within the regulatory frameworks and laws that were in force at the time.  It is true that the banks tried to, often successfully, modify the laws that governed their businesses, but this is not a crime, nor is it different than any other industry.  Moreover, while the compensation arrangements are not designed to result in long term thinking, it is true that a number of senior executives, such as Dick Fuld - Lehman's CEO, were hammered by the crash.  The fact of the matter is the banks did not do well in this crisis, which is why they needed to be bailed out.  Put differently, the banks did not profit.  If they had, they would not have needed the bail outs.  To me personally, the story of the crisis is incredibly complex and simple at the same time.  It boils down to greed.  Home buyers were extremely greedy and bought houses that they could not have reasonably expected that they could have afforded.  For example, the one homeowner in the film that describes being put into an adjustable rate mortgage that screwed them fails to describe that the loan amount was well north of $500,000.  Not many people on salaries less than $200,000 per year let alone less than $100,000 per year could reasonably expect to pay that type of mortgage regardless of whether or not it was an adjustable rate mortgage.  The fact of the matter is that they decided to buy something that they could not have expected to be able to repay.  This greed stretched up and down the social chain from the middle class home buyer, to the mortgage servicer, to the mortgage bundler, to the CDO purchaser.  Everyone was greedy and we all paid a collective price for that, but no one is willing to look in the mirror and take personal responsibility for it.  This film further perpetuates that mentality, by demonizing certain easy targets in the crisis, but it fails to take a more circumspect view of the crisis.  In point of fact, the SEC complaint against Goldman Sachs for the synthetic CDO that it sold (Timberwolf I believe) was complete crap.  A synthetic CDO is comprised of cash flows from Credit Default Swaps (CDS).  By definition, a synthetic CDO has to be made of bets against the mortgage securities underlying it, and if you look at the offering documents for this synthetic CDO it actually has a diagram showing that someone is betting against the underlying mortgage reference securities.  Nonetheless, the SEC and the news outlets, and the film, make it out as if this was some hidden fact.    The only reason that I did not give the film one star is that it did put the role of academics under a microscope, which was helpful.  It was potentially slanted, as the rest of the film clearly was, but it is an important footnote that should be understood.

0439023513|5.0|I really can't think of a more fitting ending to this series than the route Collins decided to take. Would it have really been believable for Katniss to ride off into the sunset with minimal damage after she's been through so much? I've seen a lot of reviews in which readers became disenchanted with Katniss's deteriorating drive (for lack of a better word) after being rescued from the 2nd games, but in the context of the book, she is only a 17 year old, HUMAN, girl. She's not miraculous or endowed with super powers. Naturally, neither Peeta nor Katniss would ever be the same after all the events of their life. Likewise, Collins never makes Katniss out to be a saint, either. I've seen a lot of reviews that site her inability (yes, inability) to vote \\"no\\" to the post-war Hunger Games as a weakness and character flaw on Katniss's part, but don't we live in a society that still has the death penalty for that very reason - revenge? I think the point is Katniss does the best with what she has. She truly never stops fighting, even when Peeta comes back irrevocably damaged.     I only had two issues with the book.     While I understand why Katniss voted in favor of the Hunger Games, I think that point was her chance to break from the status quo and she misses the opportunity entirely. I really have to think that Katniss would have pulled back at this point and realized that this was not the best choice. Also, how did Peeta reconcile Katniss's blood lust later in the book (Collins never really addresses that).     My second big issue was not with the way the book resolved itself, but in the manner. Certainly, Collins makes it clear that Katniss and Peeta grow together, but I think part of the resolution has to be how that happened. If she's trying to make a statement that \\"life goes on\\", wouldn't she want to specify how these two damaged people picked up the pieces? It is only on this one point that I felt Collins just got lazy and missed the mark. There are way too many unanswered questions. Why did Peeta come back? Why did he give Katniss a second chance? How did he overcome his demons? Why did Katniss ultimately decide to have kids? Was it just because Peeta wanted them or because the world was a better place? Where the heck did Haymitch go after it was all said and done? Collins hints that he (Haymitch) does interact with Peeta and Katniss, but you would think he would become a prominent figure in their lives after the war ends.     I hope that when the movie comes out (if they make the entire series) that they will consider addressing these issues (with Collins's input, of course). I think that would at least make up for the lack of detail in the final chapter.
B003UESJH4|5.0|In contrast to the Academy's puzzling choice of Best Picture in 2010 (the instantly forgettable `The Hurt Locker'), in 2011 the award of Best Picture, Best Actor, Best Director and Best Screenplay to this fine film seems to have been anticipated by all and greeted with universal acclaim.<br /><br />The story is one which most viewers will already know, so spoiler alerts ought to be unnecessary. The stammer suffered by King George VI (known as the Duke of York in public and `Bertie' in private prior to his wholly unexpected and unlooked-for coronation, following his elder brother's sudden abdication in 1936), especially whilst speaking in public, had defeated even the most able speech therapists laden with high medical credentials. Bertie's wife Elizabeth (played by Helena Bonham-Carter in one of her best-ever roles) sought out Lionel Logue, a highly unconventional Australian with no formal qualifications in speech therapy who employed self-taught, unorthodox but highly effective techniques developed whilst treating traumatised survivors of the WW1 trenches to speak again. The development of an improbable relationship of trust and mutual respect between Colin Firth's Bertie and Geoffrey Rush's Logue is at the heart of the drama; explored with intelligence, poignancy and sharp humour, it's one of the best realised screenplays of recent years.<br /><br />The first-class acting tour-de-force from the two male leads is boosted by a stellar supporting cast: the aforementioned Ms. Bonham-Carter as the Duchess of York/Queen Elizabeth; Michael Gambon in excellent form as George V (whose untimely demise initiated the constitutional crisis); Guy Pearce as the less-than-sympathetic Edward VIII; Jennifer Ehle as Lionel's wife Myrtle and Tim Spall hamming it up as Winston Churchill. Tom Hooper's direction is a textbook lesson in how to involve an audience from first frame to last by using fine character actors delivering a first-class script, snappy editing, pacy and involving storyline, witty and intelligent one-liners and a tension-packed but ultimately triumph-over-adversity-feelgood ending.<br /><br />The film has received some minor criticisms for its compression of real-life timescales: Bertie and Logue in reality began working together as early as 1926 and Bertie's stammer was much improved after only a few months; Churchill was not PM nor even in the Cabinet on the declaration of war in September 1939 and no cheering crowds greeted the King's outbreak-of-war speech, and so on. However the film is not a documentary but a piece of drama, and these few liberties are taken (as in many a decent drama) to enable it to work effectively in a 2-hour timeframe.<br /><br />The plaudits surrounding `The King's Speech' are entirely justified, as are the basket of top-level awards it's scooped. It's entertaining, clever and edge-of-seat riveting. Colin Firth's performance in particular is a marvel, and will likely be one of the best you'll see this year.<br /><br />'The King's Speech' is everything a truly great film should be, and a deserving multiple award-winner.oryline, witty and intelligent one-liners and a tension-packed but ultimately triumph-over-adversity-feelgood ending.<br /><br />The film has received some minor criticisms for its compression of real-life timescales: Bertie and Logue in reality began working together as early as 1926 and Bertie's stammer was much improved after only a few months; Churchill was not PM nor even in the Cabinet on the declaration of war in September 1939 and no cheering crowds greeted the King's outbreak-of-war speech, and so on. B004X0XA82|4.0|From the likes of The Decemberists and Bon Iver to Mumford & Sons and Blitzen Trapper, indie folk seems to be a nationwide trend especially in the pacific northwest. It pays homage to music of the 60s and 70s with a bit of a country music tinge thrown in. Seattle's Fleet Foxes are one of the forerunners of this genre and site acts like Bob Dylan, Peter, Paul & Mary, Neil Young, Crosby, Stills & Nash, and Ennio Morricone as inspiration for their music, especially their new album which releases nationwide on May 3rd.<br /><br />\\"I was raised up believing I was somehow unique... but I'd rather be a functioning cog in some great machinery\\" lead singer and primary songwriter Robin Pecknold sings on the title track of Fleet Foxes' sophomore album \\"Helplessness Blues.\\" The theme of this album, Pecknold says, \\"is the struggle between who you are and who you want to be or who you want to end up, and how sometimes you are the only thing getting in the way of that.\\"<br /><br />The theme of addressing life after newfound fame paired with formidable song arrangements have made \\"Helplessness Blues\\" a real gem to flannel-wearing bearded guys all over. Okay... other people will love it too.<br /><br />Fleet Foxes first and self-titled album was released in 2008 and their follow-up was meant to be recorded that same year. But due to an overwhelming and frankly unexpected positive reaction to the first album, subsequent touring and promotion shelved it temporarily.<br /><br />After several other unseen forces such as illness, scheduling, creative doubt, and rewriting, \\"Helplessness Blues\\" was finally finished in December of 2010. It was recorded at several places: what used to be Seattle's Hall of Justice until Death Cab For Cutie moved out, in Woodstock New York at Dreamland Recording, and finally back in Seattle at several other studios including Avast! Recording Co. where bands such as Band of Horses, Soundgarden, and The Shins have also recorded.<br /><br />Pecknold describes the album as, \\"a synthesis of folk rock, traditional folk & psychedelic pop, with an emphasis on group vocal harmonies,\\" the latter of which is in true Fleet Foxes fashion. The first album was full of bountiful and intricate vocal harmonies on songs like \\"Sun It Rises,\\" \\"Quiet Houses,\\" and \\"He Doesn't Know Why.\\" As a whole it was very echoey and delicate, yet at the same time concrete and exquisitely crafted. Every word sounds like it is being sung from the end of a long hallway with the microphone at the other end. It almost felt like an accidental success, as though the band set out to solely make music that appealed to them. But as it turns out, it appealed to many others as well, not to mention the fact that their second album taking as long as it has to release, has left people eager for more.<br /><br />Intricate vocals and harmonies are still one of the focal points of the Fleet Foxes experience with \\"Helplessness Blues.\\" From that standpoint Fleet Foxes seem to be operating under an if-it's-not-broken-don't-fix-it mentality which has the potential to get old if continually recycled but for now is working well.<br /><br />Instrumentation plays a much more significant role on this album. In addition to traditional instrumentation, the likes of much more unique sounds are present such as hammered dulcimer, zither, wood flute, tympani, tamboura, fiddle, clarinet, Tibetan singing bowls, and vibraphone.<br /><br />Although this album took much more time than intended to make and release it is easy to see that Fleet Foxes have found their niche and are looking for ways to expound upon that. They even take the time to throw in a lot of instrumentals (such as the song \\"The Cascades\\") here and there which is a welcome treat on any album.<br /><br />The title track stands out not only musically but lyrically as well. The theme of introspection and overcoming one's own limitations to find success is as appealing as any.<br /><br />The song \\"Someone You'd Admire\\" has lyricsthat are very contemplative. It is a simple tune with just Pecknold's vocals and a guitar. It is nice to see that in the midst of an album which focused a bit more on complexity that Fleet Foxes are still able to demonstrate simplicity which in turn makes the album more emotionally impacting.<br /><br />\\"The Shrine/An Argument\\" is two distinct songs in one and is the album's longest track. The first half starts off like it was taken directly from a Simon & Garfunkel album and leads into a more percussive section. The second half mellows down as far as percussion goes but introduces an interesting section where a lot of the aforementioned instrumentation of the album is present.<br /><br />Fleet Foxes are not the type of band that will initially blow you away. Rather, their music has to be chipped away layer by layer before you feel like you have a grasp on it, which is a great thing for an album's longevity. From that standpoint, their music takes effort to enjoy. It has depth and cannot be taken at face value. While \\"Helplessness Blues\\" doesn't initially stand out as a classic album I think it has the potential to grow into an album that will stand the test of time and ultimately end up on a lot of \\"Best of 2011\\" lists.vocals and a guitar. He was captured and tortured by the Nazis, deported to Buchenwald, and was within one day of being executed. After the end of the war, he played a key role in drafting the Declaration of the Universal Rights of Man for the United Nations, which was adopted in 1948. 1439102724|4.0|Sing You Home is about SO many different things. Picoult takes on a lot in this novel and I was pleasantly surprised to find that she really pulls it off. The novel is about divorce, infidelity, gay rights, fundamentalist Christians, but mostly it is a novel about love and family. It is a novel about the true meaning of family, about the fact that family comes in many shapes and sizes and that everyone deserves a chance to have that family he/she dreams of.    I am usually a fan of Jodi Picoult's books, but in recent years I've been underwhelmed with some of what she's put out there. Not so for Sing You Home. I was extremely satisfied and happy with what she did with the novel, I can safely recommend the book and she is back in my good graces.  What I liked best about the book was the character development. With over 400 pages, the reader really gets the opportunity to sink into the book and get to know the characters so it's a good thing Picoult creates such believable, interesting characters. Everyone in the book felt real to me and while I didn't particularly like all of them, each one of them added a crucial element to the story.    While Sing You Home has the typical courtroom drama aspect to it that all of Picoult's books seem to have, in this case I found it to be a small part of the book and fit in perfectly with everything else.    The pacing of this book was absolutely fantastic. I was fully immersed in the story after the first couple of chapters, and once I was about halfway through I could not put it down. Literally - I think I read the entire last half in one Sunday afternoon (something I typically don't do). And the ending worked very well for me.    Overall, I would say that Sing You Home is one of Jodi Picoult's better novels. I would definitely recommend it, whether you're a fan of hers or not.

0756404738|5.0|\\"Wise Man's Fear\\" is a book any author would be proud to call his or her own. When \\"Name of the Wind\\" first came to my attention, I read it twice before reluctantly putting it down and beginning the long wait for the next installment to appear. During that wait, I wondered what \\"Wise Man's Fear\\" would be like, and if it could hold up to the original's greatness. However, the day I came home to a large box occupying my front steps, my concerns were blown away. The book resting inside was a heavy tome packed full of what I could only hope was more or the greatness found in the first book. I sat down and began to read.    Thankfully, I had nothing to worry about in the first place. \\"Wise Man's Fear\\" immediately sucked me back into the fascinating world introduced in NoTW. There was no time wasted on recapping the previous book, and the literary connections to NoTW (ie the \\"Silence\\" passages) brought a smile to my face and created a sense of coming home.    Where NoTW was effective in introducing characters and laying out an outline of both past and present, WMF delves deeper into Kvothe's past and gives more insight to his current position, as well as elaborating a little on just who Bast is and what the extent of his relationship to 'Reshi' might be. While there are fewer Interludes in WMF, the ones that are there are more relevant to the plot now, as readers become more comfortable with the relationships and personalities of each character. The story arc that Kvothe relays has also picked up from the first book. This time young Kvothe breaks out and travels across the continent, meaning readers get to enjoy both new characters and previously-unheard-of cultures. Rothfuss again shows his mastery of creation as he lets readers explore his world from east to west and even into the Fay.    Rothfuss's writing style has maintained its greatness through the second book. It's easy to read but packed with information, sometimes requiring me to pause and think about what I hadjust read. Other times I found myself laughing rather robustly in the middle of a coffee shop or other, equally as public places. Rothfuss's comedic nature seems to ooze itself into his words, whether he realizes it or not, and it creates a delightful blend of dramatic sequences and hilarious situations.    Finally, WMF brings to the table what its predecessor could not: by now we know who the characters are. We are beginning to understand their backgrounds. At this point, we have read through some of the feats Kvothe is famous for in NoTW. All of this combines to create a surprisingly urgent desire for more. Where NoTW gave an itch to know more about the characters and plot and world, WMF leaves readers with an ache to know what happens next, how things connect with each other. Especially with the final Interlude being one of great interest for those intrigued by Bast, the wait for Rothfuss's third book will undoubtedly seem even longer than it did for WMF. Fortunately, with Rothfuss behind the words, as soon as that third book finds its way to my doorstep, the wait will have been well worth it.
B000001FKY|5.0|Released in 1987 four years after their breakthrough t album, Pyromania , Hysteria remains UK band Def Leppard's peak commercially. The band may have thought it was impossible to have a more successful album than Pyromania was but they outdid themselves and Hysteria went on to sell more copies than its predecessor. Producer Mutt Lange wanted Hysteria to be a rock version of Michael Jackson's Thriller album; every track had to be a potential single. After the events following Pyromania which was a very successful record in its own right, the band came back and was now bigger than ever selling over 12 million copies in the U.S. alone, marking a truly magnificent comeback. Some listeners may feel that this album was overproduced, especially compared to the band's earlier albums On Through The Night and High `N' Dry but back then Hysteria was current and what was cutting edge. Hysteria is a softer, not so distant, but natural evolution of Pyromania an at that point many bands had gone softer with a more commercial and pop-like direction at this point.  With its seven singles (that's right, seven) Hysteria was simply inescapable if you were alive in the late 1980's and many of those songs continue to receive considerable airplay on classic rock radio.<br /><br />Hysteria is an achievement for the band. In 1984 drummer Rick Allen lost an arm in a vehicle accident making the 3 years between Pyromania and Hysteria difficult ones for the band. The band could've went on with another drummer but they supported Rick and the young drummer developed a new technique with the help of electronic foot pedals that allowed him to reproduce sounds he would usually play with both arms. It's really incredible that Rick was determined enough to continue when he could have been devastated and quit. The band being behind him and supporting him all this time says a lot about them. In fact Joe Elliot has been quoted as saying that Allen was a better drummer with one hand than two. It took the band 3 years to release the follow up to Pyromania but after a very welcomed comeback at Donington 1986 the band knew their fans would still be there for them, and they were. The process for Hysteria took longer than albums generally do and the band went over budget. Was it worth it? You tell me.<br /><br />\\"Pour Some Sugar On Me\\" remains the song Def Leppard is most popular for, say what you want about this song being commercial and radio-friendly but try, just try not to sing along or have that chorus stuck in your hand, chances are both will eventually happen. I probably heard it too many times for my liking but it's still a great song and one of Lepp's signature songs. \\"Rocket\\" is another classic, one of the highlights of the album and one of the hardest rocking. \\"Animal\\" remains one of my favorite song from this album, it's just a fantastic song with a terrific chorus and simply put, it's the band doing what they do best. \\"Armageddon It\\" is pure Leppard, its rocking, it's catchy and its highly effective, what more could you want? Hysteria has plenty of good time rock'n'roll but it also has its soft moments:\\"Love Bites\\" is one of the best ballads Def Leppard has done, it's intense and sounds very heartfelt, it's one of my favorite songs by the band and it was a huge hit for them. The title track \\"Hysteria\\" is less a ballad than it is an anthem, it's a slow and powerful song with meaningful lyrics unlike many of the Hair/Glam bands that were popular during the 1980s.<br /><br />In 2006 a deluxe edition of Hysteria was released with an extra disc of unreleased content like B-Sides, extended versions, remixes as well as bonus and lives tracks. If you're a hardcore fan of the band or if you love this album then it might be worth it to upgrade for the deluxe edition. It's always cool for fans to get bonus unreleased content and this one has more than an entire CD devoted to them. Hysteria was well deserving of this \\"Deluxe\\" treatment!<br /><br />Hysteria is Def Leppard at the height of their success, try they would but Def Leppard would never get to this level again. Hysteria is not my favorite Def Leppard album but this was the band at the peak of their popularity and most successful. I think Hysteria remains one of the best things the band has ever done and the music is still as good today as it was back then, it stood the test of time and those songs are now classics. Interesting enough at the time of its release Hysteria was the longest rock album ever made at little less than 63 minutes, this has since changed but it was an impressive feat back then. The band would try to match the success of Hysteria with 1992's Adrenalize and subsequent releases. Hysteria marks a high point in Def Leppard's career and is an overall, excellent and very solid album continuing what they had begun a few years earlier with Pyromania. 5/5 Recommended.ss, try they would but Def Leppard would never get to this level again. B00479SVBU|5.0|After endless frustration with chapters playing out-of-order, ignoring  alpha-sort and/or numerical sort order, I called Satechi. I asked the tech if Satechi devices could use a play list. He informed me that the only \\"play list\\" the devices used was the date the file was created.    This is not the easiest tag to change in a file. When copying files and folders to SD or Flash, if the files (chapters) are small many will copy and create files with exactly the same creation date. There seems to be no \\"fall back\\" sort scheme that Satechi uses, and those files with the same date seem to play in random order.    This is a glaring fault in an mp3/Wma player that Satechi advertises as ideal for audio books. It's not.     Proper format for books would be alpha order of the files, within alpha ordered folders. It should also use standard playlists.    Other than the above, I love everything else about the SoundFly AUX, and it's still so much better than anything else for books in the car I still gave it a \\"5\\".    Today, I found what I feel to a great solution for this issue. A program called \\"AttributeMagicPro\\" (google it).    This super file utility has the capability of resetting nearly every \\"tag\\" an mp3/wma file could have, and some specialized tags for photo files as well.  Not only that, but you generate simple \\"templates\\" that govern how the attributes are changed. You can then simply recall a template and apply it to your files.    For books to be played on the SoundFly Aux, I set up a template to SEQUENTIALLY re-tag the files \\"created\\", \\"Modified\\", and \\"Accessed\\" date tags, starting with a specified date, specified time, and then incrementing the minutes by 1 minute for each file. It can also handle special tags for photo files as well.    This program can then \\"re-date\\" all date tags on one file, a thousand files, or a directory full of files AND folders in seconds.    You can direct it to sort any tag including filename before conversion, so you can order your book correctly before performing the batch re-tag. The files are modified \\"in place\\" so you can change them after they are moved to SD or Flash, or perform the modification on your hard drive first. Then copying them as a batch to your ram will not change the times enough to get them out of order.    They can then be directly copied to a SD card or Flash Ram, and playback in a completely predictably manner.    It works beautifully.  It's not \\"freeware\\", but the price of $30.00 is very reasonable considering the utility of the software. After May 7th, it goes up to $35.00. They offer a free trial, but it's limited to a \\"batch\\" of 20 files at a time.    note: the program can also modify file attributes (read-only, hidden, system, etc.) as well as tags. It's extremely flexible, and very easy to use.    I personally think Satechi should make some arrangement for all Satechi devices to ship with this software, since their scheme of playback order is so unusual for an mp3/wma player, 99% of which will use a standard play list, or play in ascii sort order. Even their in-dash AM/FM/CD player uses this non-standard ordering.

0345522478|5.0|This is based on a free Advanced Reading Edition I received for free in exchange for a review.     The plot sounds simple enough- Atticus O'Sullivan has a sword and Aenghus Og wants it, so Atticus must outwit Aenghus until the unavoidable final confrontation.     Of course, things aren't all that simple. Atticus is a two thousand year old druid, and has worked hard at staying alive for a long time. With earth magic, and friends ranging from werewolves to an elderly widow, Atticus manages to survive the various machinations of Aenghus. Aenghus himself doesn't show up until the very end, but his hand is everywhere. He is one of the Celtic gods, many of whom make an appearance in this story.  While it is clear from the beginning that Aenghus will do anything to destroy Atticus, the motivations of the other gods and goddesses that show up aren't nearly as straight forward. It leaves the reader guessing just what is happening, and what is going to happen. It makes for some delightful twists and turns throughout the pages of the book.     Atticus is a well-created character, with enough humanity to make him enjoyable to read about. He is also unique, not a copy of the characters a reader usually finds in the pages of urban fantasy. His age isn't just an afterthought, but tied into the character. His experiences are alluded to regularly, just like anyone else would talk about their past. I especially enjoyed the way old phrases were used along side modern idioms.     The various gods and goddesses are true to the tales told about them, with motivations all their own. They play with the humans, and have their own sort of moral code they live by. I've read a lot of Celtic and Nordic mythology, and I love how the gods and goddesses come alive in this story as they interact with the modern world.     I thoroughly enjoyed reading this book. It was a wonderful book to start a new series, and I highly recommend it!

B0025UHKNS|4.0|I normally don't write much, but I thought I should do this, since it was the help of many Amazon reviewers that made me decided to buy this watch. I guess it's my way of saying thank you for all the hard work I saw in these pages.    I have this watch for almost 6 months now and I normally use it to run outside, but sometimes I also use in the gym. I use it together with the Heart Rate monitor and the Foot pod. I wear it with good and bad weather, rain, snow, hot and humid temperatures, cold and dry weather, etc... I already accumulated 1000 miles and I didn't have any major issues so far.    I'm a runner, 6'1, 200 pounds. I normally run 5 to 6 times a week and the FR 405CX goes with me all the time. I'm not an expert, but I can work with a computer. It's an interesting device, but not without some issues.    When you first received, I recommend to read the instructions. It's not that difficult to operate it, but the bezel needs some time to get used to. After setting it and doing my 1st run, I connected to the Garmin program. You will have to download some programs to fully integrate with your computer, but when it's done, I didn't have any issues. It's basically straightforward. Garmin Connect it's a basic Web program and besides that there's Garmin Training Center, a primitive computer based program. I use GC most of the time to review my workouts and I leave the GTC only to make my so-called Advanced Workouts (I write more later on).    The GPS works nicely and connection is fast. I use this watch in US, Europe and Asia, without problems. If required to work indoors, the footpod is very reliable and accurate. I'd recommend it to buy it together.    During my training, the 405CX can display a myriad of information in 3 pages plus a Heart Rate page. I normally use 2 pages with Distance, lap pace, HR, Time, Time of day and Cadence. If I use the Advanced Workouts, the watch automatically adds another page with Lap Pace and Lap Distance remaining. I leave it to autoscroll and keep the Bezel locked. To be honest it's a little annoying that I might need to wait until the page shows the info I want - specially if I'm running hard - but I think it's still better than to have to deal with the bezel getting crazy because of sweat. Besides, I would prefer to have 4 fields per page as the FR310, not only 3.    The Advanced Workouts is a very nice tool to use it. Basically, you go to GTC and select what and how you want to do for your work out. You can pretty much ask for anything, like keeping a HR zone for some mins, then change your pace for some many miles, then do some Interval training, cool down, etc. and anytime, if you don't follow what you selected, there will be an alert, which helps me to maintain what I selected. I specially like to use it to keep a pace for some miles and then play with the pace.    The Heart Rate monitor is nothing special from other brands, but I've had some HR spikes sometimes (HR goes to 210 then back to 140); besides, it's a little weird that a sports watch can not be waterproof, and I don't see why the limitations for using during a swim, but that's the way Garmin had designed it.     The battery would last for 8 hrs using GPS, it seems appropriate for up to a Marathon, and for me it's enough. I normally keep the watch charged 100%, but during a 2hrs workout, with GPS, backlight on, foot pod and HR monitor enabled, it drains to 65%. If I used indoors, I disable the GPS and the battery lasts forever.    It's a little bulky to use in as a day-to-day watch, but I don't mind using it during weekends or casually. I wouldn't use in my job normally, but occasionally it would be OK too.    The price had dropped significantly after the release of FR 410 and now the new FR 610. If money is a concern and you don't want a TV on your arm (like the FR305 or the 310), then the 405CX is a nice selection.    I'm happy with the 405CX, but Garmin just released the FR610, which looks now as a better option (take a look on DCrainmaker review, it's well worth it). 6 months ago, FR 405CX was the best option for me, but if I have to buy a GPS watch today, I would recommend to spend a little more money and buy the FR 610.    I hope this review will help you in your decision.

B000001FXV|5.0|When Bob Marley played the Lyceum Theater in London in the summer of 1973, Marley was just reaching stardom. He had started out in the 1960s with The Wailers, whose original lineup included Peter Tosh and Bunny Wailer, both of whom had incredible careers in the reggae genre as well (as solo artists, of course). The sides recorded on Trojan, JAD, and Wail'Em Soul'Em were largely unnoticed by the general public. But, that would all change. Marley signed on Island Records in late 1972, and released 'Catch a Fire' not long afterward.     Marley's career began to take off. By the time he died of cancer in 1981, Marley was already a legend. He had an incredibly loyal fanbase, and I have no doubt Marley would still be on top of the world if he were alive today. In 2011, he is more popular than ever, and rightfully so. You'd be hard pressed to find anybody with more talent than Bob Marley. He could write beautifully, sing masterfully, and knew how to fire up a crowd or just a group of people (what a showman he was!). Marley was truly an immortal legend; his loss is still felt 30 years after his passing.    'Live' was recorded at the Lyceum Theater over two nights in June 1973; Marley was just becoming a superstar by this point. This was recorded for the King Biscuit Flower Hour radio show, and the disc is a document to just how great he truly was in concert. You think he's masterful in the studio (which he was)? Oh boy are you in for a treat when you hear him live. He was quite the showman (as I said above), and if you need evidence of it, look no further.    There are incredible live versions of Marley standards. The early classics 'Lively Up Yourself' and 'Trenchtown Rock' are given great reinterpretations here; I actually prefer these versions to their studio counterparts. 'Lively Up Yourself' is especially good; Marley's energy and the crowd's energy really blend together to create a truly classic version of a truly classic reggae/ska tune.    But, the one track here that EVERYONE has heard is 'No Woman No Cry.' Yes, that famous version you heard on 'Legend' and elsewhere was recorded at this show. This is the definitive version. This track has something to do with why Marley catapulted to fame; the crowd singing along turns this into almost an anthem. Marley's vocals are affected, and the lyrics are gut-wrenching. This is a truly masterful version performed in a truly masterful way.    Overall, if you like Bob Marley & The Wailers and you don't own 'Live!,' you are missing out. Buy it immediately. This is one of the all-time great live albums; no lover of great live music should be without this one. Buy it and be amazed by the spectacle of a Bob Marley concert. Bob Marley may be dead, but his music will live on for many generations to come.     Buy 'Live' and listen to a truly great concert. Enjoy the show.

The people of District 13, who had long been thought to have been wiped out in the Dark Days, are alive and helping the other districts rise up and fight a revolution against the Capitol. As a fan favorite in the games and one of the few people to publicly stick it to the Capitol, Katniss has been chosen to be the Mockingjay - the symbol of rebellion and revolution.     Life in District 13 is very different from anything she has known before. They are sticklers for rules, regulations, and every move is planned in advance. Meals are based on the person's build, fitness level, and number of minutes until the next meal. The day's schedule is stamped on your forearm at the beginning of the day with ink that is designed to last long enough until the day's end - and not a minute longer.
